        </div><div class="casAbstract">Increased interest in alternatives to lithium-ion batteries has led to promising work on sodium-ion batteries, particularly with layered oxide cathode materials.  For practical applications, however, their lower energy d. and cycle life relative to lithium-ion layered oxide cathodes make them an inadequate alternative.  A greater utilization of sodium ions with charging voltages &gt;4 V vs. Na would increase the energy d., but higher cutoff voltages cause decreased cycling stability.  To date, very little is known on the capacity fade mechanisms of layered oxide cathodes operating in the high-voltage regime.  We herein report, for the first time, the effects of extended high-voltage cycling in O3-type Na(Ni0.3Fe0.4Mn0.3)O2.  By analyzing the extended cycling performance in conjunction with X-ray diffraction, galvanostatic intermittent titrn. technique, electrochem. impedance spectroscopy, and XPS, the interconnected mechanisms of capacity fade are elucidated.  An irreversible loss of the high-voltage (OP2) phase transition above 4 V due to iron migration causes rapid capacity fade during the initial stage of cell operation.  After the disappearance of the OP2 phase, electrolyte decompn. and structural degrdn. continue to occur, leading to a significant impedance growth and faster capacity fade than cells cycled at 4 V.  This study provides valuable insight into the fundamental limitation of O3 layered oxide cathodes and offers guidelines for future materials modification.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGrwgNnPKn37FbVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lhfIZ97fgDu6g"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BB3cXhs1aqsLzI&amp;md5=5c05dff93427d9a3c546c77971a4e18b</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref9"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref9'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">9</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit9" data-doi="10.1021/acsaem.1c02485"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Lamb, J.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Manthiram, A.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Surface-Modified Na(Ni <sub>0.3</sub> Fe <sub>0.4</sub> Mn <sub>0.3</sub>)O <sub>2</sub> Cathodes with Enhanced Cycle Life and Air Stability for Sodium-Ion Batteries</span>. <i>ACS Appl.         </div><div class="casAbstract">Sodium layered-oxide cathodes show promise as an alternative to lithium layered oxides for grid-scale energy storage applications.  Among the many sodium layered-oxide chemistries, O3-type structures with a mixt. of nickel, manganese, and iron are of interest due to their high theor. energy d.  The main challenges of these materials include poor cycle life and air stability due to surface reactivity in both electrolytes and ambient moisture.  Surface modifications can mitigate both challenges by protecting the underlying cathode material.  Phosphate coatings are of particular interest due to their low cost and high cond., but they have not yet been explored for O3 sodium layered-oxide materials.  Furthermore, the relative benefits of different coating methods and phosphate groups are not well-understood.  Herein, the electrochem. performance and air stability of two coating methods are compared, and their unique challenges with an O3 sodium layered oxide are explored.  Unlike lithium layered oxides or P2-type sodium layered oxides, O3 sodium layered oxides suffer from irreversible extn. of sodium during the coating process resulting in metal oxide formation.  This sodium extn. causes reduced capacity and cond. at high coating contents and highlights the unique challenges of O3-type sodium layered oxides.  Despite these challenges, a 1% (NaPO3)n coating achieves significant improvement over the uncoated material, corresponding to a 14% gain in capacity retention after 100 cycles and only a loss of 30 mA h g-1 after 9 days in humid air, compared to 80 mA h g-1 lost in the uncoated sample.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGrXqm_oAT-derVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lhfIZ97fgDu6g"> &gt;&gt;                         cas_cit11R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">11</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">O3-type NaNi1/3Fe1/3Mn1/3O2 layered cathode for Na-ion batteries: Structural evolution and redox mechanism upon Na (de) intercalation</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Jeong, Mihee; Lee, Hayeon; Yoon, Jaesang; Yoon, Won-Sub</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Journal of Power Sources</span>

        </div><div class="casAbstract">Layered sodium-ion battery cathode, O3-type NaNi1/3Fe1/3Mn1/3O2, has been systematically investigated by synchrotron-based analyses to characterize the structural behavior during electrochem. reaction.  X-ray absorption spectroscopy shows reversible redox process upon cycling and clearly proves that both Ni and Fe are active in Na1-xNi1/3Fe1/3Mn1/3O2 and that redox couples of Ni2+/Ni4+via Ni3+ and Fe3+/Fe4+ are responsible for charge compensation.  Specifically, the capacity is mainly realized with Ni2+/Ni4+ and slightly from Fe3+/Fe4+ under charging voltage of 4.0 V.  At high voltage (&gt;4.0 V), however, Fe redox reaction is dominant and Ni contributes slightly to capacity.  In structural evolution, Na1-xNi1/3Fe1/3Mn1/3O2 undergoes phase transformation from O3 to P3 phase below 4.0 V and further reaches OP2 structure above 4.0 V along with a significant contraction of d-spacing.  Moreover, quant. anal. of extended X-ray absorption fine structure suggests that disorder of local structure for Fe is greatly increased in high voltage region.  Accordingly, collapse of d-spacing can be considered as being caused by Fe migration in the TM layer into the neighboring Na layer.  This study thus provides detailed redox behavior and factor of structural distortions under high voltage region by considering bulk and local structural changes.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGpZuP31_mXoibVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lhfIZ97fgDu6g"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC1MXhslWrtrvO&amp;md5=d6b2c6fada3a9a39867cc4738c3308d6</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref12"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref12'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">12</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit12" data-doi="10.1038/ncomms12032"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wang, J.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Yamada, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Sodeyama, K.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Chiang, C.         </div><div class="casAbstract">Finding a viable electrolyte for next-generation 5 V-class lithium-ion batteries is of primary importance.  A long-standing obstacle has been metal-ion dissoln. at high voltages.  The LiPF6 salt in conventional electrolytes is chem. unstable, which accelerates transition metal dissoln. of the electrode material, yet beneficially suppresses oxidative dissoln. of the aluminum current collector; replacing LiPF6 with more stable lithium salts may diminish transition metal dissoln. but unfortunately encounters severe aluminum oxidn.  Here we report an electrolyte design that can solve this dilemma.  By mixing a stable lithium salt LiN(SO2F)2 with di-Me carbonate solvent at extremely high concns., we obtain an unusual liq. showing a three-dimensional network of anions and solvent mols. that coordinate strongly to Li+ ions.  This simple formulation of superconcd. LiN(SO2F)2/dimethyl carbonate electrolyte inhibits the dissoln. of both aluminum and transition metal at around 5 V, and realizes a high-voltage LiNi0.5Mn1.5O4/graphite battery that exhibits excellent cycling durability, high rate capability and enhanced safety.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGr7eoyxC6XYXbVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lgEnBpCivCKpQ"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC28XhtFSqt7zN&amp;md5=d8e3f4411e273e1efa2211e84832768d</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref13"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref13'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">13</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit13" data-doi="10.1016/j.ensm.2018.04.022"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Patra, J.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Huang, H.-T.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Xue, W.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wang, C.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Helal, A. Using this electrolyte, a very high Coulombic efficiency of 99.3% at the 120th cycle for Na plating/stripping is obtained in Na/stainless steel (SS) cells with highly reduced corrosivity toward Na metal and high oxidn. durability (over 4.9 V vs. Na/Na+) without corrosion of the aluminum cathode current collector.                         cas_cit16R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">16</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Effects of ester-based electrolyte composition and salt concentration on the Na-storage stability of hard carbon anodes</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Chen, Cheng; Wu, Mengqiang; Liu, Jiahao; Xu, Ziqiang; Zaghib, Karim; Wang, Yuesheng</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Journal of Power Sources</span>

        </div><div class="casAbstract">Hard carbon (HC) is the most feasible anode for Na-ion batteries (NIBs).  However, HC electrodes suffer from low initial coulombic efficiency (ICE), poor rate capability and cyclic instability.  Here, a systematic investigation was carried out to understand the effect of Na salt, esters-based solvent and concn. on the electrochem. performance of hard carbon spheres (HCSs) electrodes.                         cas_cit17R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">17</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Fire-extinguishing organic electrolytes for safe batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Wang, Jianhui; Yamada, Yuki; Sodeyama, Keitaro; Watanabe, Eriko; Takada, Koji; Tateyama, Yoshitaka; Yamada, Atsuo</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Nature Energy</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2018</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">3</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">1</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">22-29</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">NEANFD</span>;

            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Nature Research</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Severe safety concerns are impeding the large-scale employment of lithium/sodium batteries.  Conventional electrolytes are highly flammable and volatile, which may cause catastrophic fires or explosions.  Efforts to introduce flame-retardant solvents into the electrolytes have generally resulted in compromised battery performance because those solvents do not suitably passivate carbonaceous anodes.  Here we report a salt-concd. electrolyte design to resolve this dilemma via the spontaneous formation of a robust inorg. passivation film on the anode.  We demonstrate that a concd. electrolyte using a salt and a popular flame-retardant solvent (tri-Me phosphate), without any additives or soft binders, allows stable charge-discharge cycling of both hard-carbon and graphite anodes for more than 1,000 cycles (over one year) with negligible degrdn.; this performance is comparable or superior to that of conventional flammable carbonate electrolytes.  The unusual passivation character of the concd. electrolyte coupled with its fire-extinguishing property contributes to developing safe and long-lasting batteries, unlocking the limit toward development of much higher energy-d. batteries.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGqh5VD_9omOWrVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lgEnBpCivCKpQ"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC1cXitVehtbc%253D&amp;md5=f17e94b071fcc675467c969b0b2e28ef</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref18"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref18'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">18</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit18" data-doi="10.1149/1945-7111/ab75fd"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Fondard, J.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Irisarri, E.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Courrèges, C.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Palacin, M.                         cas_cit18R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">18</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">SEI composition on hard carbon in Na-Ion batteries after long cycling: Influence of salts (NaPF6, NaTFSI) and Additives (FEC, DMCF)</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Fondard, J.; Irisarri, E.; Courreges, C.; Palacin, M.         </div><div class="casAbstract">A study of the solid electrolyte interphase (SEI) on hard-carbon (HC) electrodes in sodium half-cells is presented.  Electrochem. performances over &gt; 100 cycles were compared with two different salts (NaPF6, NaTFSI) and two different electrolyte additives (FEC, DMCF) in a mixt. of EC and DMC solvents.  The best electrochem. performances were obsd. with NaPF6 salt in conjunction with 3% FEC.  The DMCF additive had a detrimental effect in all electrolyte combinations.  The chem. characterization of the SEI was carried out by XPS and showed that the best electrochem. behavior was related to an SEI compn. based on sodium ethylene dicarbonate and NaF, whereas poorer electrochem. performances were assocd. to either low NaF or high Na2CO3 content.  The results reported herein provide an insight on the SEI chem. on hard carbon electrodes in sodium cells after long-term cycling, as a complement to previous studies dealing with the first cycles.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGq4rxL0JhE-c7Vg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0li3MAvSB_yYUA"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BB3cXosVKisL0%253D&amp;md5=fc871aceb661bd6af19f8153b66817bd</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref19"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref19'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">19</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit19" data-doi="10.1016/j.nanoen.2016.09.013"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Cao, R.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Mishra, K.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Li, X.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Qian, J.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Engelhard, M.         </div><div class="casAbstract">Rechargeable batteries based upon sodium (Na+) cations are at the core of many new battery chemistries beyond Li-ion batteries.         </div><div class="casAbstract">The approaches enhancing the energy d. of lithium (Li)-ion batteries (LIBs) often push the batteries to their safety limit.  Therefore, electrolytes that not only enhance electrochem. performances but also improve safety properties of LIBs are urgently needed for further development of LIBs.  Although org. phosphorus-contg. solvents have been used with org. carbonates to form flame-retarding or nonflammable electrolytes, their poor compatibility with the graphite anodes leads to fast capacity fade of LIBs.  Herein a promising approach is reported by formulating a nonflammable localized high-concn. electrolyte based on tri-Et phosphate, ethylene carbonate, and a partially fluorinated ether. As a result, this electrolyte shows good compatibility with both the graphite anode and the high-voltage cathode and enables long-term cycling stability of the graphite||NMC811 cells.  This work sheds new light in the development of nonflammable electrolytes for high-energy-d. LIBs.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGpsxAHAgZ_cXLVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0li3MAvSB_yYUA"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC1MXhvVWjsLjJ&amp;md5=ecbe9c193461f4952d9f9a4043bde4a3</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref21"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref21'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">21</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit21" data-doi="10.1021/acsenergylett.0c01712"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Jin, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Xu, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Le, P.                         cas_cit21R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">21</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Highly Reversible Sodium Ion Batteries Enabled by Stable Electrolyte-Electrode Interphases</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Jin, Yan; Xu, Yaobin; Le, Phung M. L.; Vo, Thanh D.; Zhou, Quan; Qi, Xingguo; Engelhard, Mark H.; Matthews, Bethany E.; Jia, Hao; Nie, Zimin; Niu, Chaojiang; Wang, Chongmin; Hu, Yongsheng; Pan, Huilin; Zhang, Ji-Guang</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">ACS Energy Letters</span>

        </div><div class="casAbstract">The sodium ion battery (NIB) is a promising alternative technol. for energy storage systems because of the abundance and low cost of sodium in the Earth's crust.  However, the limited cycle life and safety concerns of NIBs hinder their large-scale applications.  Here, we report a nonflammable localized high concn. electrolyte (sodium bis(fluorosulfonyl)imide-triethyl phosphate/1,1,2,2-tetrafluoroethyl-2,2,3,3-tetrafluoropropyl ether (1:1.5:2 in molar ratio)) for highly reversible NIBs.  By using a cryo-transmission electron microscope, it was found that an ultrathin (3 nm) and robust interphase layer formed on the cathode surface can block transition metal dissolns. and minimize surface reconstructions of the cathode.  The inorg.-rich solid electrolyte interphase formed on the hard carbon (HC) surface minimized undesirable reactions between HC and the electrolyte.  These stable interphases enabled high Coulombic efficiency and long-term stable cycling of the HC anode and the NaCu1/9Ni2/9Fe1/3Mn1/3O2 cathode.  The insights obtained in this work can be used to further improve the cycling stability and safety of rechargeable batteries.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGpNJOYnP_xlILVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0li3MAvSB_yYUA"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BB3cXhvVertbfO&amp;md5=b9e1a3721be8297ac106f551772c8740</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref22"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref22'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">22</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit22" data-doi="10.1021/acsenergylett.7b01213"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zheng, J.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Chen, S.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhao, W.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Song, J.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Engelhard, M.                         cas_cit22R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">22</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Extremely Stable Sodium Metal Batteries Enabled by Localized High-Concentration Electrolytes</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Zheng, Jianming; Chen, Shuru; Zhao, Wengao; Song, Junhua; Engelhard, Mark H.; Zhang, Ji-Guang</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">ACS Energy Letters</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2018</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">3</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">2</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">315-321</span>CODEN:

        </div><div class="casAbstract">Sodium (Na) metal is a promising anode for Na-ion batteries.  However, the high reactivity of Na metal with electrolytes and the low Na metal cycling efficiency have limited its practical application in rechargeable Na metal batteries.  High-concn. electrolytes (HCE, ≥4 M) consisting of sodium bis(fluorosulfonyl)imide (NaFSI) and ether solvent could ensure the stable cycling of Na metal with high Coulombic efficiency but at the cost of high viscosity, poor wettability, and high salt cost.  Here, we report that the salt concn. could be significantly reduced (≤1.5 M) by a hydrofluoroether as an "inert" diluent, which maintains the solvation structures of HCE, thereby forming a localized high-concn. electrolyte (LHCE).  A LHCE [2.1 M NaFSI/1,2-dimethoxyethane (DME)-bis(2,2,2-trifluoroethyl) ether (BTFE) (solvent molar ratio 1:2)] enables dendrite-free Na deposition with a high Coulombic efficiency of &gt;99%, fast charging (20C), and stable cycling (90.8% retention after 40000 cycles) of Na‖Na3V2(PO4)3 batteries.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGrBQZaeDCQNKLVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0li3MAvSB_yYUA"> &gt;&gt;                         cas_cit23R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">23</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Stable sodium metal anode enhanced by advanced electrolytes with SbF3 additive</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Fang, Wei; Jiang, Rui; Zheng, Hao; Zheng, Yi; Sun, Yi; Liang, Xin; Xiang, Hong-Fa; Feng, Yue-Zhan; Yu, Yan</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Rare Metals (Beijing, China)</span>

    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">40</span>

        </div><div class="casAbstract">The practical application of sodium metal batteries (SMBs) is hampered due to the inferior interfacial stability between Na metal and conventional electrolytes.  Therefore, a high-concn. electrolyte is proposed to solve this issue.  However, high viscosity, low ionic cond., and unsatisfactory wettability toward the separator need to be overcome.  In this study, a localized high-concn. electrolyte (LHCE) is formulated with 1 wt% SbF3 as an interface-stabilized additive to protect the Na metal anode.  This reformulated LHCE retains the special coordination structure in HCE with improved wettability and high ionic cond.  Moreover, the introduction of the SbF3 additive into the LHCE resulted in a bilayer-structured solid electrolyte interface (SEI) including a Na-Sb alloy inner layer and a NaF-rich outer layer on the Na metal.  As expected, the Na||Na cells using LHCE + 1 wt% SbF3 show a long cycle lifespan of over 1200 h at 0.5 mA·cm-2 with negligible polarization, and Na||Na3V2(PO4)3 cells exhibit a high capacity exceeding 97 mAh·g-1 at 40C.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGqNfI7ub-imX7Vg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0liYpQaAsvco6Q"> &gt;&gt;                         cas_cit24R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">24</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Enhanced Sodium Metal/Electrolyte Interface by a Localized High-Concentration Electrolyte for Sodium Metal Batteries: First-Principles Calculations and Experimental Studies</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Wang, Yueda; Jiang, Rui; Liu, Yongchao; Zheng, Hao; Fang, Wei; Liang, Xin; Sun, Yi; Zhou, Rulong; Xiang, Hongfa</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">ACS Applied Energy Materials</span>

        </div><div class="casAbstract">The applications of Na metal batteries (SMBs) are restricted owing to the capacity attenuation and safety hazards during the cycling process, while a rational design of the electrolyte is crit. on solving this problem.  In this work, an electrolyte is designed by adding 1,1,2,2-tetrafluoroethyl-2,2,3,3-tetrafluoropropylether (TTE) into a 3.8 M sodium bis(fluorosulfonyl)imide/1,2-dimethoxyethane (NaFSI/DME) electrolyte, forming the localized high-concn. electrolyte (LHCE) for constructing a stable solid electrolyte interface (SEI) for SMBs.  Ab initio mol. dynamics (AIMD) results indicate that the solvation degree of Na+ ions with DME mols. in LHCE is lower than that in HCE, which leads to more FSI- anions but less DME mols. to decomp. on the Na metal anode.  And the TTE could also decomp. on the Na metal anode, which synergistically builds a NaF-rich compact SEI with low surface resistance and good mech. property so that it is favorable for the transportation of Na+ ions and suppression of the Na dendrite growth.  Therefore, the optimized LHCE electrolyte in SMBs exhibits an outstanding electrochem. performance.  This study provides an updated perspective on the understanding and design of localized high-concn. electrolytes for SMBs.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGpSYEd-REOZZLVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0liYpQaAsvco6Q"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BB3MXhsFaqsL7E&amp;md5=7c69af4109946956dfbfbc0d51b6ad4f</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref25"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref25'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">25</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit25" data-doi="10.1039/D1EE03103A"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Su, L.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Charalambous, H.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Cui, Z.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Manthiram, A.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">High-Efficiency, Anode-Free Lithium–Metal Batteries with a Close-Packed Homogeneous Lithium Morphology</span>. <i>Energy Environ.                         cas_cit25R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">25</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">High-efficiency, anode-free lithium-metal batteries with a close-packed homogeneous lithium morphology</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Su, Laisuo; Charalambous, Harry; Cui, Zehao; Manthiram, Arumugam</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Energy &amp; Environmental Science</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2022</span>),

            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Royal Society of Chemistry</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Anode-free lithium-metal batteries (LMBs) are ideal candidates for high-capacity energy storage as they eliminate the need for a conventional graphite electrode or excess lithium-metal anode.  Current anode-free LMBs suffer from low Coulombic efficiency (CE) due to poor lithium stripping efficiency.  Advanced electrolyte development is a promising route to maximize lithium plating and stripping CE and minimize capacity fade.  However, a poor understanding of the mechanisms by which advanced electrolytes improve performance hampers progress in the practical development of anode-free LMBs.  Here, we use synchrotron techniques and other tools to analyze the influence of three com. available electrolytes on the compn., heterogeneity, kinetics, morphol., and electrochem. of anode-free LMBs.  Advanced electrolytes improve the electrochem. performance of anode-free LMBs by forming much denser and better-packed Li morphologies on a Cu current collector than on the conventional electrolyte.  Li plates uniformly over the electrode area with the advanced electrolytes rather than in a few active sites.  Inactive cryst. Li with heterogeneous distribution dominates the capacity degrdn. of anode-free cells, esp. with the conventional electrolyte, indicating that reducing the amt. of "dead" cryst. Li will significantly improve the cycling stability of anode-free cells.  The understanding of the Li plating and stripping process obtained from this work will accelerate the development of anode-free LMBs with high efficiency.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGrc6_XMlW7397Vg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0liYpQaAsvco6Q"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BB38XhsFCqsbY%253D&amp;md5=69fc515744696225c4fc9ee1927002c4</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref26"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref26'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">26</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit26" data-doi="10.1002/aenm.202103806"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Pan, R.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Cui, Z.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Yi, M.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Xie, Q.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Manthiram, A.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Ethylene Carbonate-Free Electrolytes for Stable, Safer High-Nickel Lithium-Ion Batteries</span>. <i>Adv.                         cas_cit26R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">26</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Ethylene Carbonate-Free Electrolytes for Stable, Safer High-Nickel Lithium-Ion Batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Pan, Ruijun; Cui, Zehao; Yi, Michael; Xie, Qiang; Manthiram, Arumugam</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Advanced Energy Materials</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2022</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">12</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">19</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">2103806</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">ADEMBC</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">1614-6840</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Wiley-Blackwell</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Ethylene carbonate (EC) is an important component in state-of-the-art electrolytes for lithium-ion batteries (LIBs).  However, EC is highly susceptible to oxidn. on the surface of high-nickel layered oxide cathodes, making it undesirable for next-generation high-energy-d. LIBs.  In this study, a simple, yet effective, EC-free electrolyte (20F1.5M-1TDI) is presented by adding 20 wt% fluoroethylene carbonate (FEC) and 1 wt% lithium 4,5-dicyano-2-(trifluoromethyl)imidazole (LiTDI) into 1.5 M LiPF6 in an Et Me carbonate (EMC) electrolyte.  The 20F1.5M-1TDI electrolyte is found to efficiently passivate the graphite anode and stabilize high-nickel cathodes by a synergistic decompn. of FEC and LiTDI.  The LiNi0.9Mn0.05Al0.05O2 (NMA90)/graphite full cell with the 20F1.5M-1TDI electrolyte, therefore, exhibits an enhanced cycling stability and a suppressed voltage hysteresis growth compared to that with an EC-contg. baseline electrolyte (1 M LiPF6 in EC:EMC, 3:7 in wt., with 2 wt% vinyl carbonate).  Advanced anal. tools, such as time-of-flight secondary ion mass spectrometry and XPS, are employed to understand the underlying working mechanism of the EC-free electrolyte.  The present study clearly showcases the great potential of EC-free electrolytes as a straightforward, practical approach for LIBs with high-nickel cathodes.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGqiVIVltXe65bVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0liYpQaAsvco6Q"> &gt;&gt;                         cas_cit27R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">27</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Armoring LiNi1/3Co1/3Mn1/3O2 Cathode with Reliable Fluorinated Organic-Inorganic Hybrid Interphase Layer toward Durable High Rate Battery</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Chen, Yu; Zhao, Weimin; Zhang, Quanhai; Yang, Guangzhi; Zheng, Jianming; Tang, Wei; Xu, Qunjie; Lai, Chunyan; Yang, Junhe; Peng, Chengxin</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Advanced Functional Materials</span>

        </div><div class="casAbstract">Ternary layered oxide materials have attracted extensive attention as a promising cathode candidate for high-energy-d. lithium-ion batteries.  However, the undesirable electrochem. degrdn. at the electrode-electrolyte interface definitively shortens the battery service life.  An effective and viable approach is proposed for improving the cycling stability of the LiNi1/3Co1/3Mn1/3O2 cathode using lithium difluorophosphate (LiPO2F2) paired with fuoroethylene carbonate (FEC) as co-additives into conventional electrolytes.  It is found that the co-additives can greatly reduce the interface charge transfer impedance and significantly extend the life span of LiNi1/3Co1/3Mn1/3O2//Li (NMC//Li) batteries.  The developed cathode demonstrates exceptional capacity retention of 88.7% and remains structural integrity at a high current of 5C after 500 cycles.  Fundamental mechanism study indicates a dense, stable fluorinated org.-inorg. hybrid cathode-electrolyte interphase (CEI) film derived from LiPO2F2 in conjunction with FEC additives on the surface of NMC cathode material, which significantly suppresses the decompn. of electrolyte and mitigates the dissoln. of transition metal ions.  The interfacial engineering of the electrode materials stabilized by the additives manipulation provides valuable guidance for the development of advanced cathode materials.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGppBtC216Vr0bVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0liYpQaAsvco6Q"> &gt;&gt;                         cas_cit28R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">28</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Non-flammable electrolyte enables Li-metal batteries with aggressive cathode chemistries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Fan, Xiulin; Chen, Long; Borodin, Oleg; Ji, Xiao; Chen, Ji; Hou, Singyuk; Deng, Tao; Zheng, Jing; Yang, Chongyin; Liou, Sz-Chian; Amine, Khalil; Xu, Kang; Wang, Chunsheng</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Nature Nanotechnology</span>

        </div><div class="casAbstract">Rechargeable Li-metal batteries using high-voltage cathodes can deliver the highest possible energy densities among all electrochemistries.  However, the notorious reactivity of metallic lithium as well as the catalytic nature of high-voltage cathode materials largely prevents their practical application.  Here, we report a non-flammable fluorinated electrolyte that supports the most aggressive and high-voltage cathodes in a Li-metal battery.  Our battery shows high cycling stability, as evidenced by the efficiencies for Li-metal plating/stripping (99.2%) for a 5 V cathode LiCoPO4 (∼99.81%) and a Ni-rich LiNi0.8Mn0.1Co0.1O2 cathode (∼99.93%).         </div><div class="casAbstract">Sodium metal batteries (SMBs) are promising for large scale energy storage due to the remarkable capacity of sodium metal anode (SMA) and the natural abundance of Na-contg. resources.         </div><div class="casAbstract">Cathode materials control both the energy d. and cost of Li-ion and Li-metal batteries.  The cobalt-free LiNiO2 with relatively low cost and extremely high theor. energy d. (∼1,050 Wh kg-1) is one of the most promising cathode materials for high-energy batteries.  However, the continuous Ni dissoln., structural disordering, particle cracking, and unstable cathode electrolyte interphase (CEI) hinder its applications.  Here, we surmount these challenges by forming a robust fluoride (F)- and boron (B)-rich CEI on LiNiO2 using a high-fluorinated electrolyte with LiDFOB additive.  The LiNiO2 cathode maintains an unprecedentedly high capacity retention of &gt;80% after 400 deep cycles at a high charge cut-off voltage of 4.4 V (vs. Li/Li+).  In addn., the electrolyte forms an F- and B-rich interphase on the Li metal and graphite anodes, allowing stable cycling of full cells.  This work sheds light on designing interfacial chem. for high-energy cathodes, and its principle is applicable for other alkali metal ion cathodes.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGozD2fPKcqk47Vg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lgbzEj86xCY2w"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC1MXhvFynu77E&amp;md5=004cdcdc7441e052092437d3d1315a2a</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref31"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref31'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">31</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit31" data-doi="10.1021/acsenergylett.1c01714"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Langdon, J.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Cui, Z.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Manthiram, A.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Role of Electrolyte in Overcoming the Challenges of LiNiO <sub>2</sub> Cathode in Lithium Batteries</span>. <i>ACS Energy Lett.</i>  <span class="NLM_year" style="font-weight: bold;">2021</span>,  <span class="NLM_volume"><i>6</i></span>,  <span class="NLM_fpage">3809</span>– <span class="NLM_lpage">3816</span>, <span class="refDoi">&nbsp;DOI:

                        cas_cit31R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">31</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Role of Electrolyte in Overcoming the Challenges of LiNiO2 Cathode in Lithium Batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Langdon, Jayse; Cui, Zehao; Manthiram, Arumugam</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">ACS Energy Letters</span>

        </div><div class="casAbstract">LiNiO2 (LNO) is a high-capacity and model cathode first discovered in the 1980s that fell out of favor due to its intrinsic instabilities.  However, research activities toward LNO are once again on the rise as the push for higher-energy-d. cells marches on.  We demonstrate here that with appropriate modern electrolytes, major performance improvements can be achieved with LNO with no addnl. modifications.  Cells with a localized high concn. electrolyte (LHCE) deliver 92% capacity retention after 200 cycles compared with 56% capacity retention in a baseline carbonate electrolyte, maintain 94% capacity after high-voltage storage compared with 77% capacity, and display a higher onset temp. of thermal runaway of 244°C compared with 188°C.  These improvements are attributed to the LHCE's high oxidative stability and its formation of fluorine-rich interphases.  Although further characterization of this new class of electrolyte is necessary, this work demonstrates that modern electrolytes can be drop-in enablers of high-capacity, long-cycle-life cells.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGpi6e7-Xfq0LLVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lgbzEj86xCY2w"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BB3MXitFOmsbnF&amp;md5=298b5f46ff20ac39062192dd5739ba81</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref32"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref32'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">32</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit32" data-doi="10.1021/acs.chemmater.0c02435"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Lamb, J.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Manthiram, A.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Synthesis Control of Layered Oxide Cathodes for Sodium-Ion Batteries: A Necessary Step Toward Practicality</span>. <i>Chem. Mater.</i>  <span class="NLM_year" style="font-weight: bold;">2020</span>,  <span class="NLM_volume"><i>32</i></span>,  <span class="NLM_fpage">8431</span>– <span class="NLM_lpage">8441</span>, <span class="refDoi">&nbsp;DOI:
                10.1021/acs.chemmater.0c02435</span> </span><div class=" links-group getFTR"><div class="getFTR__content getFTR__placeholder"></div><span class="references__suffix"><a class="google-scholar" href="/action/getFTRLinkout?url=http%3A%2F%2Fscholar.google.com%2Fscholar_lookup%3Fhl%3Den%26volume%3D32%26publication_year%3D2020%26pages%3D8431-8441%26journal%3DChem.%2BMater.%26author%3DJ.%2BLamb%26author%3DA.%2BManthiram%26title%3DSynthesis%2BControl%2Bof%2BLayered%2BOxide%2BCathodes%2Bfor%2BSodium-Ion%2BBatteries%253A%2BA%2BNecessary%2BStep%2BToward%2BPracticality%26doi%3D10.1021%252Facs.chemmater.0c02435&amp;doi=10.1021%2Facsami.2c05402&amp;doiOfLink=10.1021%2Facs.chemmater.0c02435&amp;linkType=gs&amp;linkLocation=Reference&amp;linkSource=FULL_TEXT" target="_blank">Google Scholar</a></span><div class="casRecord" id="
                        cas_cit32R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">32</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Synthesis Control of Layered Oxide Cathodes for Sodium-Ion Batteries: A Necessary Step Toward Practicality</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Lamb, Julia; Manthiram, Arumugam</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Chemistry of Materials</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2020</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">32</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">19</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">8431-8441</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">CMATEX</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">0897-4756</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">American Chemical Society</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Interest in sodium-ion battery cathode materials, particularly for grid-scale energy-storage applications, has considerably increased in the last few years, sparking a push toward industrially practical research.  In addn. to more full-cell and pouch-cell research, there has also been more study on the use of an industrial synthesis technique for producing sodium-based cathode materials.  Hydroxide copptn. is the industry std. for synthesizing lithium-based layered-oxide cathode materials because it is scalable and produces materials with highly tunable particle morphol. with low impurity.  The tuning of particle morphol. allows for improved energy d. and reduced surface reactivity, leading to better stability in air and electrolyte.  Despite these benefits, the ability to synthesize sodium-based layered-oxide cathode materials with beneficial particle morphol. is considerably more challenging than it is for lithium-based layered oxides due to the complexity of numerous interconnected variables.  We herein provide a review of the hydroxide copptn. method for both sodium- and lithium-based cathode materials, highlighting its benefits and the need for further studies on utilizing copptd. sodium-based layered-oxide materials.  We then perform an in-depth anal. utilizing a combination of literature, fundamental chem., and exptl. data to discuss the challenges of hydroxide copptn. and provide a framework for overcoming these challenges.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGosifkOzKyUyLVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lgbzEj86xCY2w"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BB3cXitVeisrzJ&amp;md5=5edfe0970f6fb50b5f2486e7abf823d0</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref33"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref33'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">33</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit33" data-doi="10.1016/j.jpowsour.2018.08.025"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Che, H.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Yang, X.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wang, H.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Liao, X.-Z.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhang, S. S.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wang, C.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Ma, Z.-F.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Long Cycle Life of Sodium-Ion Pouch Cell Achieved by Using Multiple Electrolyte Additives</span>. <i>J. Power Sources</i>  <span class="NLM_year" style="font-weight: bold;">2018</span>,  <span class="NLM_volume"><i>407</i></span>,  <span class="NLM_fpage">173</span>– <span class="NLM_lpage">179</span>, <span class="refDoi">&nbsp;DOI:
                10.1016/j.jpowsour.2018.08.025</span> </span><div class=" links-group getFTR"><div class="getFTR__content getFTR__placeholder"></div><span class="references__suffix"><a class="google-scholar" href="/action/getFTRLinkout?url=http%3A%2F%2Fscholar.google.com%2Fscholar_lookup%3Fhl%3Den%26volume%3D407%26publication_year%3D2018%26pages%3D173-179%26journal%3DJ.%2BPower%2BSources%26author%3DH.%2BChe%26author%3DX.%2BYang%26author%3DH.%2BWang%26author%3DX.-Z.%2BLiao%26author%3DS.%2BS.%2BZhang%26author%3DC.%2BWang%26author%3DZ.-F.%2BMa%26title%3DLong%2BCycle%2BLife%2Bof%2BSodium-Ion%2BPouch%2BCell%2BAchieved%2Bby%2BUsing%2BMultiple%2BElectrolyte%2BAdditives%26doi%3D10.1016%252Fj.jpowsour.2018.08.025&amp;doi=10.1021%2Facsami.2c05402&amp;doiOfLink=10.1016%2Fj.jpowsour.2018.08.025&amp;linkType=gs&amp;linkLocation=Reference&amp;linkSource=FULL_TEXT" target="_blank">Google Scholar</a></span><div class="casRecord" id="
                        cas_cit33R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">33</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Long cycle life of sodium-ion pouch cell achieved by using multiple electrolyte additives</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Che, Haiying; Yang, Xinrong; Wang, Hong; Liao, Xiao-Zhen; Zhang, Sheng S.; Wang, Chunsheng; Ma, Zi-Feng</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Journal of Power Sources</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2018</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">407</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue"></span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">173-179</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">JPSODZ</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">0378-7753</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Elsevier B.V.</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Sodium-ion pouch cells with up to 1000 cycles are presented by using a NaNi1/3Fe1/3Mn1/3O2 cathode, a hard carbon anode, and a functional electrolyte.  The functional electrolyte is composed of 1 M NaPF6 dissolved in a 1:1 (vol./vol.) mixed solvent of propylene carbonate (PC) and Et Me carbonate (EMC) with 3-4 wt% of two or three additives, including fluoroethylene carbonate (FEC), prop-1-ene-1,3-sultone (PST), and 1,3,2-Dioxathiolane-2,2-dioxide (DTD).  It is shown that the capacity retentions of the cells increase to 84.4% and 92.2% after 1000 cycles for electrolytes contg. FEC-PST bi-additive and FEC-PST-DTD tri-additive, resp., as compared with that contg. FEC single additive.  Using XPS, inductively coupled plasma optical, and transmission electron microscopy, post-mortem analyses on the surface of the cycled electrodes indicate that PST and DTD are beneficial to the anode by forming an org. compd. rich solid electrolyte interphase (SEI), and to the cathode by forming a dense and thick cathode electrolyte interphase (CEI) that consequently prevents transition metal ions from dissolving into electrolyte.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGq-8YJtx9tWOLVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lh-iyn6uhvZOg"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC1cXhsFahtLnI&amp;md5=5fc47b41d2211cc84d4e027d0f0b8670</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref34"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref34'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">34</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit34" data-doi="10.1021/acs.jpcc.7b12140"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Hwang, J.-Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Myung, S.-T.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Sun, Y.-K.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Quaternary Transition Metal Oxide Layered Framework: O3-Type Na[Ni <sub>0.32</sub> Fe <sub>0.13</sub> Co <sub>0.15</sub> Mn <sub>0.40</sub> ]O <sub>2</sub> Cathode Material for High-Performance Sodium-Ion Batteries</span>. <i>J. Phys. Chem. C</i>  <span class="NLM_year" style="font-weight: bold;">2018</span>,  <span class="NLM_volume"><i>122</i></span>,  <span class="NLM_fpage">13500</span>– <span class="NLM_lpage">13507</span>, <span class="refDoi">&nbsp;DOI:
                10.1021/acs.jpcc.7b12140</span> </span><div class=" links-group getFTR"><div class="getFTR__content getFTR__placeholder"></div><span class="references__suffix"><a class="google-scholar" href="/action/getFTRLinkout?url=http%3A%2F%2Fscholar.google.com%2Fscholar_lookup%3Fhl%3Den%26volume%3D122%26publication_year%3D2018%26pages%3D13500-13507%26journal%3DJ.%2BPhys.%2BChem.%2BC%26author%3DJ.-Y.%2BHwang%26author%3DS.-T.%2BMyung%26author%3DY.-K.%2BSun%26title%3DQuaternary%2BTransition%2BMetal%2BOxide%2BLayered%2BFramework%253A%2BO3-Type%2BNa%255BNi%2B0.32%2BFe%2B0.13%2BCo%2B0.15%2BMn%2B0.40%2B%255DO%2B2%2BCathode%2BMaterial%2Bfor%2BHigh-Performance%2BSodium-Ion%2BBatteries%26doi%3D10.1021%252Facs.jpcc.7b12140&amp;doi=10.1021%2Facsami.2c05402&amp;doiOfLink=10.1021%2Facs.jpcc.7b12140&amp;linkType=gs&amp;linkLocation=Reference&amp;linkSource=FULL_TEXT" target="_blank">Google Scholar</a></span><div class="casRecord" id="
                        cas_cit34R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">34</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Quaternary Transition Metal Oxide Layered Framework: O3-Type Na[Ni0.32Fe0.13Co0.15Mn0.40]O2 Cathode Material for High-Performance Sodium-Ion Batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Hwang, Jang-Yeon; Myung, Seung-Taek; Sun, Yang-Kook</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Journal of Physical Chemistry C</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2018</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">122</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">25</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">13500-13507</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">JPCCCK</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">1932-7447</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">American Chemical Society</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Analogous compds. in lithium-ion batteries (LIBs), various ternary chem. compns. in O3-type layered oxides, have been introduced in sodium-ion batteries (SIBs).  However, O3-type ternary transition metal oxide cathodes, including the NaNixCoyMnzO2 and NaNixFeyMnzO2 (x + y + z = 1) compds., continue to face several challenges with respect to their low reversible capacity and poor cycle retention owing to their structural instability.  Herein, we propose the well-balanced quaternary transition metal oxide structure of O3-type Na[Ni0.32Fe0.13Co0.15Mn0.40]O2 as cathode materials that have an av. compn. of both Na[Ni0.25Fe0.25Mn0.5]O2 and Na[Ni0.4Co0.3Mn0.3]O2 compds.  Compared to its resp. ternary members, the Na[Ni0.32Fe0.13Co0.15Mn0.40]O2 cathode exhibits a higher specific capacity as well as improved cycling stability and rate capability.  The post-mortem ex-situ X-ray diffraction (XRD) studies of a cycled electrode clearly show that coexistence of quaternary transition metals in a Na[Ni0.32Fe0.13Co0.15Mn0.40]O2 cathode could improve the structural stability.  Moreover, quaternary transition metal oxide frameworks effectively prevent the dissoln. of transition metals during cycling, thus improving the battery performances.  The appealing phys. properties and electrochem. performance of this material demonstrate its great promise for a high-performance O3-type cathode in sodium-ion batteries.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGqHbvW6EKdvbbVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lh-iyn6uhvZOg"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC1cXktlGiu74%253D&amp;md5=bee338c33dd75d3de0cf70438e781812</span></div></div></div></div></li></ol></div><script type="text/javascript" nonce="90da89136a85b325-SJC">window.refViewer={'ref1':['cit1'],'ref2':['cit2'],'ref3':['cit3'],'ref4':['cit4'],'ref5':['cit5'],'ref6':['cit6'],'ref7':['cit7'],'ref8':['cit8'],'ref9':['cit9'],'ref10':['cit10'],'ref11':['cit11'],'ref12':['cit12'],'ref13':['cit13'],'ref14':['cit14'],'ref15':['cit15'],'ref16':['cit16'],'ref17':['cit17'],'ref18':['cit18'],'ref19':['cit19'],'ref20':['cit20'],'ref21':['cit21'],'ref22':['cit22'],'ref23':['cit23'],'ref24':['cit24'],'ref25':['cit25'],'ref26':['cit26'],'ref27':['cit27'],'ref28':['cit28'],'ref29':['cit29'],'ref30':['cit30'],'ref31':['cit31'],'ref32':['cit32'],'ref33':['cit33'],'ref34':['cit34']}</script><div class="articleCitedByDropzone" data-pb-dropzone="articleCitedByDropzone"><div class="cited-by"><div class="article_content-header cited-content_header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div tabindex="0" class="article_content-title"><h2 id="citeThis">Cited By</h2><div class="article__copy"> <button class="article__copy__btn"><i aria-hidden="true" class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><div class="cited-by__content"><div class="sciteMetricsDropzone">

        </div><div class="casAbstract">A review.  Abstr.: Lithium-ion batteries have aided the portable electronics revolution for nearly three decades.  They are now enabling vehicle electrification and beginning to enter the utility industry.  The emergence and dominance of lithium-ion batteries are due to their higher energy d. compared to other rechargeable battery systems, enabled by the design and development of high-energy d. electrode materials.  Basic science research, involving solid-state chem. and physics, has been at the center of this endeavor, particularly during the 1970s and 1980s.  With the award of the 2019 Nobel Prize in Chem. to the development of lithium-ion batteries, it is enlightening to look back at the evolution of the cathode chem. that made the modern lithium-ion technol. feasible.  This review article provides a reflection on how fundamental studies have facilitated the discovery, optimization, and rational design of three major categories of oxide cathodes for lithium-ion batteries, and a personal perspective on the future of this important area.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGpMra5MR0oCsrVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lh6M-H66MBQAg"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BB3cXlvFGnu78%253D&amp;md5=b22ad36cb85609ef702f570cf096eef0</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref2"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref2" class="rightTabRefNumLink">2</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit2" data-doi="10.1038/s41560-019-0513-0"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Li, W.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Erickson, E.         </div><div class="casAbstract">A review.  High-nickel layered oxide cathode materials will be at the forefront to enable longer driving-range elec. vehicles at more affordable costs with lithium-based batteries.  A continued push to higher energy content and less usage of costly raw materials, such as cobalt, while preserving acceptable power, lifetime and safety metrics, calls for a suite of strategic compositional, morphol. and microstructural designs and efficient material prodn. processes.  In this Perspective, we discuss several important design considerations for high-nickel layered oxide cathodes that will be implemented in the automotive market for the coming decade.  We outline various intrinsic restraints of maximizing their energy output and compare current/emerging development roadmaps approaching low-/zero-cobalt chem.  Materials prodn. is another focus, relevant to driving down costs and addressing the practical challenges of high-nickel layered oxides for demanding vehicle applications.  We further assess a series of stabilization techniques on their prospects to fulfill the aggressive targets of vehicle electrification.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGpwV_fNhbfgrrVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lh6M-H66MBQAg"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BB3cXhtF2itrs%253D&amp;md5=d876f1ce2f1fbc0599dda5d99dc05436</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref3"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref3" class="rightTabRefNumLink">3</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit3" data-doi="10.1021/cr500192f"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Yabuuchi, N.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Kubota, K.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Dahbi, M.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Komaba, S.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Research Development on Sodium-Ion Batteries</span>. <i>Chem.                         rightTab-cas_cit4R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">4</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Sodium-ion batteries: present and future</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Hwang, Jang-Yeon; Myung, Seung-Taek; Sun, Yang-Kook</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Chemical Society Reviews</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2017</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">46</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">12</span>),

            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Royal Society of Chemistry</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Energy prodn. and storage technologies have attracted a great deal of attention for day-to-day applications.  In recent decades, advances in lithium-ion battery (LIB) technol. have improved living conditions around the globe.  LIBs are used in most mobile electronic devices as well as in zero-emission electronic vehicles.  However, there are increasing concerns regarding load leveling of renewable energy sources and the smart grid as well as the sustainability of lithium sources due to their limited availability and consequent expected price increase.  Therefore, whether LIBs alone can satisfy the rising demand for small- and/or mid-to-large-format energy storage applications remains unclear.  To mitigate these issues, recent research has focused on alternative energy storage systems.  Sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) are considered as the best candidate power sources because sodium is widely available and exhibits similar chem. to that of LIBs; therefore, SIBs are promising next-generation alternatives.  Recently, sodiated layer transition metal oxides, phosphates and org. compds. have been introduced as cathode materials for SIBs.  Simultaneously, recent developments have been facilitated by the use of select carbonaceous materials, transition metal oxides (or sulfides), and intermetallic and org. compds. as anodes for SIBs.  Apart from electrode materials, suitable electrolytes, additives, and binders are equally important for the development of practical SIBs.  Despite developments in electrode materials and other components, there remain several challenges, including cell design and electrode balancing, in the application of sodium ion cells.  In this article, we summarize and discuss current research on materials and propose future directions for SIBs.  This will provide important insights into scientific and practical issues in the development of SIBs.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGot-2IKXj-Q-7Vg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lgqlYQdDnsO_A"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC2sXkvF2rsbk%253D&amp;md5=cfc7b2d153696dcbded22d954ff06174</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref5"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref5" class="rightTabRefNumLink">5</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit5" data-doi="10.1038/natrevmats.2018.13"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Vaalma, C.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Buchholz, D.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Weil, M.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Passerini, S.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">A Cost and Resource Analysis of Sodium-Ion Batteries</span>. <i>Nature Reviews Mater.</i>  <span class="NLM_year" style="font-weight: bold;">2018</span>,  <span class="NLM_volume"><i>3</i></span>,  <span class="NLM_fpage">18013</span>, <span class="refDoi">&nbsp;DOI:

            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">American Chemical Society</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Because of the low cost and high abundance of Na, room-temp. Na-ion batteries have recently been considered as an alternative power source to Li-ion batteries.  In contrast to the electrochem. performance of the batteries, safety has been paid much less attention, but safety is a crit. consideration because Na-ion batteries are intended for large-scale electrochem. energy storage applications.  Here, we report a NaNi1/3Fe1/3Mn1/3O2/hard C full cell with a good cycling performance and high Coulombic efficiency.  The energy d. of this pouch cell is ∼95 Wh/kg, and the capacity retention of the NFM full cell attained at 92.6% after 100 cycle nos.  We further used accelerating rate calorimetry, SEM, and operando synchrotron high-energy x-ray diffraction to study the thermal/chem. stability of charged NaxNi1/3Fe1/3Mn1/3O2 cathode material at both cell and component level.  It is found that the thermal decompn. of desodiated NaxNi1/3Fe1/3Mn1/3O2 is a redox reaction that can be facilitated with the presence of either a reductive environment, such as electrolytes, or a strong oxidative environment that can result from a higher degree of desodiation.         </div><div class="casAbstract">Increased interest in alternatives to lithium-ion batteries has led to promising work on sodium-ion batteries, particularly with layered oxide cathode materials.  For practical applications, however, their lower energy d. and cycle life relative to lithium-ion layered oxide cathodes make them an inadequate alternative.  A greater utilization of sodium ions with charging voltages &gt;4 V vs. Na would increase the energy d., but higher cutoff voltages cause decreased cycling stability.  To date, very little is known on the capacity fade mechanisms of layered oxide cathodes operating in the high-voltage regime.  We herein report, for the first time, the effects of extended high-voltage cycling in O3-type Na(Ni0.3Fe0.4Mn0.3)O2.  By analyzing the extended cycling performance in conjunction with X-ray diffraction, galvanostatic intermittent titrn. technique, electrochem. impedance spectroscopy, and XPS, the interconnected mechanisms of capacity fade are elucidated.  An irreversible loss of the high-voltage (OP2) phase transition above 4 V due to iron migration causes rapid capacity fade during the initial stage of cell operation.  After the disappearance of the OP2 phase, electrolyte decompn. and structural degrdn. continue to occur, leading to a significant impedance growth and faster capacity fade than cells cycled at 4 V.  This study provides valuable insight into the fundamental limitation of O3 layered oxide cathodes and offers guidelines for future materials modification.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGrwgNnPKn37FbVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lgqlYQdDnsO_A"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BB3cXhs1aqsLzI&amp;md5=5c05dff93427d9a3c546c77971a4e18b</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref9"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref9" class="rightTabRefNumLink">9</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit9" data-doi="10.1021/acsaem.1c02485"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Lamb, J.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Manthiram, A.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Surface-Modified Na(Ni <sub>0.3</sub> Fe <sub>0.4</sub> Mn <sub>0.3</sub>)O <sub>2</sub> Cathodes with Enhanced Cycle Life and Air Stability for Sodium-Ion Batteries</span>. <i>ACS Appl.         </div><div class="casAbstract">Sodium layered-oxide cathodes show promise as an alternative to lithium layered oxides for grid-scale energy storage applications.  Among the many sodium layered-oxide chemistries, O3-type structures with a mixt. of nickel, manganese, and iron are of interest due to their high theor. energy d.  The main challenges of these materials include poor cycle life and air stability due to surface reactivity in both electrolytes and ambient moisture.  Surface modifications can mitigate both challenges by protecting the underlying cathode material.  Phosphate coatings are of particular interest due to their low cost and high cond., but they have not yet been explored for O3 sodium layered-oxide materials.  Furthermore, the relative benefits of different coating methods and phosphate groups are not well-understood.  Herein, the electrochem. performance and air stability of two coating methods are compared, and their unique challenges with an O3 sodium layered oxide are explored.  Unlike lithium layered oxides or P2-type sodium layered oxides, O3 sodium layered oxides suffer from irreversible extn. of sodium during the coating process resulting in metal oxide formation.  This sodium extn. causes reduced capacity and cond. at high coating contents and highlights the unique challenges of O3-type sodium layered oxides.  Despite these challenges, a 1% (NaPO3)n coating achieves significant improvement over the uncoated material, corresponding to a 14% gain in capacity retention after 100 cycles and only a loss of 30 mA h g-1 after 9 days in humid air, compared to 80 mA h g-1 lost in the uncoated sample.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGrXqm_oAT-derVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lgqlYQdDnsO_A"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BB3MXitFCls7fO&amp;md5=92a2581fcded69a67f8d0968cbfb9ef5</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref10"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref10" class="rightTabRefNumLink">10</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit10" data-doi="10.1002/aenm.201601306"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Xie, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wang, H.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Xu, G.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wang, J.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Sheng, H.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Chen, Z.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Ren, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Sun, C.-J.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wen, J.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wang, J.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Miller, D.         </div><div class="casAbstract">Layered sodium-ion battery cathode, O3-type NaNi1/3Fe1/3Mn1/3O2, has been systematically investigated by synchrotron-based analyses to characterize the structural behavior during electrochem. reaction.  X-ray absorption spectroscopy shows reversible redox process upon cycling and clearly proves that both Ni and Fe are active in Na1-xNi1/3Fe1/3Mn1/3O2 and that redox couples of Ni2+/Ni4+via Ni3+ and Fe3+/Fe4+ are responsible for charge compensation.  Specifically, the capacity is mainly realized with Ni2+/Ni4+ and slightly from Fe3+/Fe4+ under charging voltage of 4.0 V.  At high voltage (&gt;4.0 V), however, Fe redox reaction is dominant and Ni contributes slightly to capacity.  In structural evolution, Na1-xNi1/3Fe1/3Mn1/3O2 undergoes phase transformation from O3 to P3 phase below 4.0 V and further reaches OP2 structure above 4.0 V along with a significant contraction of d-spacing.  Moreover, quant. anal. of extended X-ray absorption fine structure suggests that disorder of local structure for Fe is greatly increased in high voltage region.  Accordingly, collapse of d-spacing can be considered as being caused by Fe migration in the TM layer into the neighboring Na layer. Moreover, this ultraconcd. electrolyte exhibits good compatibility with high-voltage Na4Fe3(PO4)2(P2O7) and Na0.7(Fe0.5Mn0.5)O2 cathodes charged to high voltages (&gt; 4.2 V vs. Na/Na+), resulting in outstanding cycling stability (high reversible capacity of 109 mAh g-1 over 300 cycles for the Na/Na4Fe3(PO4)2(P2O7) cell) compared with the conventional dil. electrolyte, 1 M NaPF6 in ethylene carbonate/propylene carbonate (5/5, vol./vol.).</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGqJcuiMMc-6g7Vg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lhqMszPvXWBNw"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC2sXms1OmsQ%253D%253D&amp;md5=3a3ed93745395ec94acd0706e9658d00</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref15"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref15" class="rightTabRefNumLink">15</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit15" data-doi="10.1063/5.0005397"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Åvall, G.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Johansson, P.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">A Novel Approach to Ligand-Exchange Rates Applied to Lithium-Ion Battery and Sodium-Ion Battery Electrolytes</span>. <i>J.         </div><div class="casAbstract">Hard carbon (HC) is the most feasible anode for Na-ion batteries (NIBs).
